Wang and colleagues utilized <small><sup>7</sup></small>Li nuclear NMR to investigate the state of lithium in porous carbon discharged to 0.0 V and below 0.0 V (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig8">Fig. 8e</a>).<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit106"><sup><span class="sup_ref">106</span></sup></a> The <small><sup>7</sup></small>Li resonance around 0 ppm was found to arise from both carbon-Li compounds and Li-ions, indicating Li-ion intercalation between graphite layers and Li-ion adsorption. However, the <small><sup>7</sup></small>Li NMR results revealed no signal shift pertaining to the fill of lithium into pores until the porous carbon was discharged below 0.0 V (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig8">Fig. 8f</a>). Therefore, they proposed an adsorption-intercalation mechanism.</p>
      
      
        
        <h3 id="sect627"><span class="b_heading">3.5 The differences in electrochemical behaviors between HC-LIBs and HC-SIBs</span></h3>
        <span>The physicochemical properties of HC-LIBs and HC-SIBs are similar, but the galvanostatic discharge/charge curves for HC-LIBs and HC-SIBs exhibit significant differences.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit74"><sup><span class="sup_ref">74,107,108</span></sup></a> Both display a sloping region at high voltage and plateau regions located near 0.1 V for Na-ions and near 0 V for Li-ions. Typically, at low HTT, HC-LIBs only display a sloping capacity, along with considerable voltage hysteresis. As the HTT increases to a medium level, the sloping capacity decreases, while the plateau capacity increases. However, as the HTT continues to rise, HC-LIB displays a sloping capacity only, but with a significantly reduced value compared to lower HTT (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig9">Fig. 9a</a>). On the other hand, HC-SIBs exhibit both sloping and plateau capacity throughout the temperature range, with the sloping region having dominance at low HTT, and the plateau capacity becoming dominant at high HTT. The ration of plateau capacity keeps going, but its value decreases (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig9">Fig. 9b</a>). Notably, there exists an appropriate temperature value that provides the highest capacity.</span>
        <br><div class="image_table"><table><tbody><tr><td colspan="3" class="imgHolder" id="imgfig9"><a href="/image/article/2023/EE/d3ee02202a/d3ee02202a-f9_hi-res.gif" title="Select to open image in new window" onclick="open(this.href, &quot;_blank&quot;, &quot;toolbar=1,scrollbars=yes,resizable=1&quot;); return false;"><img alt="image file: d3ee02202a-f9.tif" src="/image/article/2023/EE/d3ee02202a/d3ee02202a-f9.gif"></a></td></tr><tr><td class="pushTitleRight"> </td><td class="image_title"><b>Fig. 9 </b> <span id="fig9"><span class="graphic_title">(a) The general pattern of changes in plateau/sloping-capacity with increasing temperature in HC-LIBs. (b) The general pattern of changes in plateau/sloping-capacity with increasing temperature in HC-SIBs. (c) and (d) show the differences in the formation and properties of the SEI. (e) and (f) show the 3 electrochemical behaviors for both Li-ion and Na-ion in HC. (g) The mechanism of Li-ion in HC from low-HTT to high-HTT. (g) and (h) show the differences in inner-pore storage for both Li-ion and Na-ion in HC.</span></span></td><td class="pushTitleLeft"> </td></tr></tbody></table></div>
        <p class="otherpara">These electrochemical differences can be attributed to several reasons, as summarized in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig9">Fig. 9</a>. Firstly, these differences are due to the composition and morphology of their respective SEI films.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit93"><sup><span class="sup_ref">93</span></sup></a> The SEI film in HC-LIBs is thin and uniform, composed mainly of organic material (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig9">Fig. 9c</a>), leading to a lower charge resistance for Li-ions and a better rate performance. In contrast, HC-SIBs form a thick and rough SEI film (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig9">Fig. 9d</a>), composed mainly of inorganic material, leading to greater resistance for Na-ions and a poor rate performance. A systematic study conducted by Li <span class="italic">et al.</span><a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit109"><sup><span class="sup_ref">109</span></sup></a> has revealed significant differences in the transport of Li-ions and Na-ions across the SEI, and impedance studies were conducted to illustrate the differential behavior of Li-ions and Na-ions across the SEI. The results indicated that Li-ions had a lower migration impedance than Na-ions.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit110"><sup><span class="sup_ref">110</span></sup></a> They believe that the rate performance of Li/Na-ion is determined by the energy barrier to cross the SEI's inorganic components. The lowest activation energy barriers calculated by density functional theory (DFT) show that Li-ion migration has a lower activation potential barrier than Na-ion migration. The rate performance of HC is largely dependent on the properties of the SEI. Therefore, more attention should be paid to SEI interface engineering to improve the rate performance of HC-SIBs in the future.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit29"><sup><span class="sup_ref">29</span></sup></a> The electrochemical behaviors of Li-ions and Na-ions in the carbon matrix exhibit notable differences. Li-ions bind more strongly to the carbon matrix,<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit111"><sup><span class="sup_ref">111,112</span></sup></a> making it challenging to penetrate into the inner space (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig9">Fig. 9e</a>), which leads to a certain level of irreversible capacity. However, with increasing HTT, when the number of defects decreases, Li-ions encounter less hindrance while entering the inner space. Nevertheless, at high HTT, the closure of nanopores can result in the formation of embedded fullerenes, which can prevent Li-ion penetration into the carbon.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit98"><sup><span class="sup_ref">98</span></sup></a> Thus, only the open pores that remain unchanged act as the sites for Li-ion storage, creating a plateau capacity.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit55"><sup><span class="sup_ref">55,83</span></sup></a> In contrast to HC-LIBs, HC-SIBs exhibit plateau capacity across the low to high-temperature range. Na-ions can easily penetrate the interior from the carbon-based surface (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig9">Fig. 9f</a>), allowing them to enter enclosed pore structures. Meanwhile a recent study concluded that the mechanism by which close-pores store Li/Na-ions is similar,<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit89"><sup><span class="sup_ref">89</span></sup></a> with the main difference being their properties during surface electrochemical behavior prior to insertion into the inner close-pore. In the case of Li-ions, they enter the inner space through insertion into the layer spacing. They then diffuse further into the inner space and eventually fill the close-pore. However, the insertion process causes a barrier for Li-ions to enter the inner space. This results in an insignificant plateau capacity. On the other hand, due to the larger ion size, Na-ions are more likely to be adsorbed onto the surface structural defects rather than intercalation. This leads to a more significant adsorption process before Na-ions enter the close-pore and the small proportion of intercalation facilitates the easy entry of Na-ions into the inner space, resulting in a plateau capacity dominated electrochemical behavior. Further understanding of the mechanisms involved in storing Li-ions and Na-ions is crucial, especially considering the ongoing controversy surrounding this topic. This area of research has significant importance to designing high-performance HC anodes. Generally speaking, achieving optimal capacity in both HC-LIBs and HC-SIBs requires tailoring the pore structure, particularly the pore-entrance diameter (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig9">Fig. 9g and h</a>). In HC-LIBs, it is crucial to tailor the pore-entrance diameter accordingly.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit113"><sup><span class="sup_ref">113</span></sup></a> But there is debate surrounding 2 strategies: (1) increasing the bulk-chemisorption sites and decreasing the intercalation capacities based on the mechanism of Lim <span class="italic">et al.</span><a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit89"><sup><span class="sup_ref">89</span></sup></a> (2) To avoid close-pore formation based on the insight that close-pore is inaccessible for Li-ions.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit83"><sup><span class="sup_ref">83</span></sup></a> In contrast, Na-ions can be stored in the closed pores undebated, and improving capacity can be achieved by creating closed pores and adjusting the pore-entrance diameter.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit110"><sup><span class="sup_ref">110</span></sup></a> The closed pore structure is ideal for the storage of Na-ions since it selectively allows Na-ions to pass through while excluding solvent molecules, thereby reducing the sites of electrolyte decomposition. However, the high HTT required for closed-pore formation can lead to over-graphitization, which must be taken into consideration.</p>
      
    
    
      
      <h2 id="sect657"><span class="a_heading">4 Strategies and perspectives based on half cells towards practical full cells</span></h2>
      <span>The initial cycle of HC-LIBs and HC-SIBs presents a significant capacity loss, and two theories explain its main cause: some researchers attribute it to the strong trapping effect of Li/Na-ions by defects and their irreversible intercalation between the graphene layers,<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit68"><sup><span class="sup_ref">68,114</span></sup></a> while others believe it is due to the formation of an unsatisfactory SEI.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit115"><sup><span class="sup_ref">115</span></sup></a> To mitigate irreversible losses, while ensuring good cycle and rate performance, it is crucial to establish an appropriate SEI.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit28"><sup><span class="sup_ref">28</span></sup></a> A desirable SEI film should be thin and dense, allowing only Li/Na-ions to pass through while preventing co-solvent molecules from penetrating. This can be achieved by improving the electrode material, optimizing the electrolyte, and developing an artificial SEI. And looking at it practically, in order to cater to the demands for applications such as power vehicles, and energy storage, it is crucial to develop electrode materials that have both a long lifespan and good rate performance, and some of the effective strategies have been explored solely from a laboratory perspective, such as structural design, doping, and composites. These have all been proven to be effective in HC-LIBs and HC-SIBs. However, perhaps the most significant factor affecting rate and cycling performance is the process of crossing the electrolyte and the SEI engineering. Moving forward, it is crucial to focus on SEI interface engineering and electrolyte optimization, in addition to continued material improvements, to further enhance full-cell performance. It is noted that, due to the more popular trend in HC-SIBs, additional optimization strategies including the tailoring of pore structure and specific structural design, such as optimizing electrolyte, all show significant improvements. However, it is worth investigating whether these strategies can also play an important role in HC-LIBs in the future. Additionally, we must address the challenges that arise when transitioning from laboratory research to commercialization, such as optimizing the slurry preparation and coating processes and matching positive and negative electrodes. By addressing these issues, we can ensure that the promising laboratory results are translated into practical and efficient commercial applications, ultimately advancing the commercialization of HC anodes.</span>
      
        
        <h3 id="sect664"><span class="b_heading">4.1 Solid electrolyte interface engineering in HC-SIBs and HC-LIBs</span></h3>
        <span>Strategies for modifying the electrode to assist in the formation of a thin and dense SEI film are encouraging. When using reduced graphene oxide (rGO) as an anode in SIB, the high chemical reactivity of the numerous defects in rGO can cause uncontrollable electrolyte decomposition, leading to a low ICE of 58.9% at 0.1 A g<small><sup>1</sup></small>. To address this issue, Yang <span class="italic">et al.</span> successfully adsorbed Al<small><sup>3+</sup></small> on defective sites in rGO by immersing it in an AlCl<small><sub>3</sub></small> solution,<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit116"><sup><span class="sup_ref">116</span></sup></a> resulting in rGO with Al<small><sub>2</sub></small>O<small><sub>3</sub></small> nanoclusters formed on defective sites in subsequent carbonization (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig10">Fig. 10a</a>). This led to an improved ICE of 70.2% at 0.1 A g<small><sup>1</sup></small> by attenuating the chemical activity of the defects and suppressing the irreversible and hazardous decomposition of electrolyte, thus improving the ICE arising from the formation of thin and homogeneous SEI.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit117"><sup><span class="sup_ref">117</span></sup></a> In another approach, Cao <span class="italic">et al.</span> used direct atomic layer deposition to deposit an ultra-thin and uniform Al<small><sub>2</sub></small>O<small><sub>3</sub></small> film that is chemically bonded to the HC,<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit118"><sup><span class="sup_ref">118</span></sup></a> which reduces the accessible SSA for electrolyte decomposition. Additionally, the artificial film also promotes the formation of a thin and robust SEI film with more organic components (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig10">Fig. 10b</a>), preventing electrolyte permeation and reducing side reactions compared to bare electrode material. The optimized HC anode with the Al<small><sub>2</sub></small>O<small><sub>3</sub></small> film has a higher reversible capacity of 355 mA h g<small><sup>1</sup></small> and a higher ICE of 75%, compared to pure HC with a reversible capacity of 260.9 mA h g<small><sup>1</sup></small> and an ICE of 67%. These studies demonstrate the potential of modifying the electrode to facilitate the formation of an effective SEI film. Strategies for electrolyte optimization to form an ideal SEI film are also encouraging. This can be achieved by adjusting the concentration of electrolyte salts, which participate in the SEI formation. For example, Xing <span class="italic">et al.</span> tailored the concentration of NaPF<small><sub>6</sub></small> in a diglyme-based electrolyte to form a discontinuous SEI,<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit119"><sup><span class="sup_ref">119</span></sup></a> which allows for co-intercalation of Na<small><sup>+</sup></small>-ether (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig10">Fig. 10c</a>). The mechanism of Na-ion storage involves Na<small><sup>+</sup></small>-ether adsorption on the surface, followed by Na<small><sup>+</sup></small>-ether co-intercalation into the carbon layer in the sloping region, which requires a clean surface. When the voltage change is below 0.1 V, filling in the pore occurs only when the surface is covered with SEI. This is because the tough surface of the pore generates a stronger electric field than the graphitic layers, inducing electrolyte decomposition. However, higher concentrations of NaPF<small><sub>6</sub></small> form a dense layer, which greatly hinders co-intercalation of Na<small><sup>+</sup></small>-ether, while lower concentrations result in a SEI-free surface, which is unfavorable for Na<small><sup>+</sup></small> filling in the pore. The optimized SEI, with a concentration of 0.2 M NaPF<small><sub>6</sub></small>, provides a discharge capacity of 459.7 mA h g<small><sup>1</sup></small> at 0.1C and a maximum ICE of 81.5%. Another effective approach for enhancing ICE is the use of an artificial SEI film. This can be achieved through pretreatments such as chemical presodiation, in which alkali metals react with the electrode material in the electrolyte. For example, lithium pre-treated HC forms a thin SEI film mixed with lithium and sodium, which reduces the participation of irreversible Na-ions in SEI formation. Furthermore, the lithium-based SEI is not dense enough to prevent Na-ions from passing through, resulting in a significant improvement in ICE from 70.3% to 92.1% compared to pure HC.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit120"><sup><span class="sup_ref">120</span></sup></a> Qian <span class="italic">et al.</span><a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit121"><sup><span class="sup_ref">121</span></sup></a> describe a way to improve the ICE by using a chemical reaction to presodiate HC by treating with a sodiation reagent called Na-Bp (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig10">Fig. 10d</a>), and the anode can be presodiated rapidly to form the presodiated HC (Na<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>HC), which will form a thin and compact SEI after immersion in electrolyte. In addition, the use of sodium diphenyl ketone (Na-DK) as a reducing reagent on HC can transfer the surface oxygenated functionalities to the sodium counterparts,<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit122"><sup><span class="sup_ref">122</span></sup></a> compensating for the irreversible defects such as dangling bonds and vacancies, and forming quasi-metallic Na. This strategy reduces the irreversible active sites and the Na-ions that participate in SEI formation, resulting in an optimized HC with a high ICE of up to 99.2% compared to 84.5% for pure HC. Moreover, the presodiation process triggers the formation of a thin, robust, and uniform film of inorganics such as NaF, as shown in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig10">Fig. 10e</a>, which contributes to the advanced rate performance, attributed to the SEI's ability to favor interfacial reaction kinetics.</span>
        <br><div class="image_table"><table><tbody><tr><td colspan="3" class="imgHolder" id="imgfig10"><a href="/image/article/2023/EE/d3ee02202a/d3ee02202a-f10_hi-res.gif" title="Select to open image in new window" onclick="open(this.href, &quot;_blank&quot;, &quot;toolbar=1,scrollbars=yes,resizable=1&quot;); return false;"><img alt="image file: d3ee02202a-f10.tif" src="/image/article/2023/EE/d3ee02202a/d3ee02202a-f10.gif"></a></td></tr><tr><td class="pushTitleRight"> </td><td class="image_title"><b>Fig. 10 </b> <span id="fig10"><span class="graphic_title">The strategies to improve ICE by forming a desirable SEI from the electrode material, electrolyte optimization and artificial method. (a) Formation of Al<small><sub>2</sub></small>O<small><sub>3</sub></small> nanoclusters on defective rGO to attenuate the chemical activity of the defects and suppress electrolyte decomposition. Reproduced with permission.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit116"><sup><span class="sup_ref">116</span></sup></a> Copyright 2018, Wiley-VCH Verlag GmbH &amp; Co. KGaA, Weinheim. (b) The optimized interface of ALD-Al<small><sub>2</sub></small>O<small><sub>3</sub></small> HC compared to bare HC. Reproduced with permission.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit118"><sup><span class="sup_ref">118</span></sup></a> Copyright 2019, Elesiver. (c) The likely explanation for how NaPF<small><sub>6</sub></small> concentration impacts the electrochemical capabilities is through three energy storage mechanisms, a non-uniform distribution model of electric field on the HC electrode, and the generation of SEI in ether-based electrolytes with varying salt concentrations. These factors correspond to the capacity contribution of the HC anode. Reproduced with permission.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit119"><sup><span class="sup_ref">119</span></sup></a> Copyright 2023, Elsevier. (d) A diagrammatic representation depicting the process of chemical presodiation and the subsequent formation of solid electrolyte interface (SEI) on the HC. Reproduced with permission.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit121"><sup><span class="sup_ref">121</span></sup></a> Copyright 2020, American Chemical Society. (e) The formation of SEI with and without pre-sodiation results in varying morphological differences. Reproduced with permission.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit122"><sup><span class="sup_ref">122</span></sup></a> Copyright 2022, Wiley-VCH GmbH.</span></span></td><td class="pushTitleLeft"> </td></tr></tbody></table></div>
        <p class="otherpara">The ICE can be improved by increasing the HTT, but this improvement comes with higher electrical resistance, poorer rate and cycle performance,<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit60"><sup><span class="sup_ref">60</span></sup></a> increased costs, and other process difficulties. To meet the demands of HC-LIBs for high-rate applications, the HTT should be kept low while developing sloping-dominated HC that has sufficient adsorption sites and guarantees an ICE of above 90%. This can be optimized through various interfacial engineering, including coating a protective layer, forming an artificial SEI layer, and optimizing the electrolyte to form a thin and dense SEI layer.</p>
        <p class="otherpara">Coating plays a significant role in reducing the sites of electrolyte decomposition<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit123"><sup><span class="sup_ref">123</span></sup></a> and blocking irreversible sites,<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit124"><sup><span class="sup_ref">124</span></sup></a> and a more conductive coating leads to improved conductivity.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit125"><sup><span class="sup_ref">125</span></sup></a> The Li<small><sub>4</sub></small>Ti<small><sub>5</sub></small>O<small><sub>12</sub></small> (LTO) coating layer completely covers the surface of HC without changing its structure (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig11">Fig. 11a</a>),<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit123"><sup><span class="sup_ref">123</span></sup></a> reducing the direct contact area with the electrolyte, which minimizes the irreversible loss of Li-ions. Finally, a thinner SEI is formed in the initial cycle, resulting in a high ICE of 90.6% (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig11">Fig. 11b</a>). Chemical vapor deposition (CVD) is another effective strategy for avoiding excessive electrolyte decomposition, and the carbon layer at the pore entrance screens out solvent molecules while allowing Li-ions to pass through with a low energy barrier (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig11">Fig. 11c</a>).<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit126"><sup><span class="sup_ref">126</span></sup></a> Traditionally, Li metal has been used as a counter-electrode and source of Li to compensate for the loss of active Li-ions. However, the increasing cost and lack of replicability in full-cell batteries have hindered commercial development. To address this issue, chemical pre-lithiation using Li-containing reagents with a strong reduction ability has shown great potential for industrial application.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit127"><sup><span class="sup_ref">127129</span></sup></a> This involves transferring Li-ions to anode materials through redox reactions. In a recent study, Qu <span class="italic">et al.</span><a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit128"><sup><span class="sup_ref">128</span></sup></a> used Libiphenyltetrahydrofuran (LiBpTHF) as the pre-lithiation reagent. By immersing a HC electrode in LiBpTHF for varying amounts of time, they were able to adjust the degree of pre-lithiation. When paired with a LiNi<small><sub>1/3</sub></small>Co<small><sub>1/3</sub></small>Mn<small><sub>1/3</sub></small>O<small><sub>2</sub></small> cathode, the full-cell exhibited significantly improved ICE (90.2 <span class="italic">vs.</span> 75%), attributed to the formation of the adjusted SEI layer (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig11">Fig. 11d</a>). In addition, chemical pre-lithiation is a simple process that has great potential for industrial application. Pre-lithiated in HC compensate for the irreversible Li-ion loss and help to form an improved SEI that lowers charge transfer resistance (<span class="italic">R</span><small><sub>ct</sub></small>), resulting in a thinner and more robust SEI that is well stable during the cycle. Ai <span class="italic">et al.</span><a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit127"><sup><span class="sup_ref">127</span></sup></a> immersed HC in a solution of Li-Naph/DME (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig11">Fig. 11e</a>), where lithium naphthalenide (Li-Naph) reacted with HC first, followed by DME decomposition to form an SEI covering the prelithiated HC (PHC), finally resulting in a thinner, denser, and more robust SEI. TEM showed that the SEI formed on PHC was only 2 nm thick (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig11">Fig. 11f</a>), compared to the 7 nm thick, loose SEI formed on pure HC (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig11">Fig. 11g</a>). Thanks to the pre-formed SEI, there was a significant reduction in the loss of Li-ions from the side reaction and participation in SEI formation, resulting in a greatly improved ICE from 75.5% to 99.5%. Although at a high current density of 4000 mA g<small><sup>1</sup></small>, the PHC had a high capacity of 194.8 mA h g<small><sup>1</sup></small>, with a capacity retention of 94.1% after 500 cycles at 400 mA g<small><sup>1</sup></small>, whereas pristine HC had only 137.7 mA h g<small><sup>1</sup></small> and 76.5%, respectively. When assembled into a full-cell, the energy density of PHC/LiCoO<small><sub>2</sub></small> was 388.1 W h kg<small><sup>1</sup></small>, which was comparable to that of the current commercial graphite/LiCoO<small><sub>2</sub></small>. It is noteworthy that the immersion time is a crucial parameter for obtaining the best results, and excessive prelithiation can lead to lithium deposition and safety issues.</p>
        <br><div class="image_table"><table><tbody><tr><td colspan="3" class="imgHolder" id="imgfig11"><a href="/image/article/2023/EE/d3ee02202a/d3ee02202a-f11_hi-res.gif" title="Select to open image in new window" onclick="open(this.href, &quot;_blank&quot;, &quot;toolbar=1,scrollbars=yes,resizable=1&quot;); return false;"><img alt="image file: d3ee02202a-f11.tif" src="/image/article/2023/EE/d3ee02202a/d3ee02202a-f11.gif"></a></td></tr><tr><td class="pushTitleRight"> </td><td class="image_title"><b>Fig. 11 </b> <span id="fig11"><span class="graphic_title">(a) When Li<small><sub>4</sub></small>Ti<small><sub>5</sub></small>O<small><sub>12</sub></small> is coated onto the surface of HC, a thinner SEI layer formed compared to pristine HC as illustrated. (b) Comparing the electrochemical performance of the initial ten cycles between LTO-coated HC and untreated HC. (a) and (b) Reproduced with permission.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit123"><sup><span class="sup_ref">123</span></sup></a> Copyright 2021, Elesiver. (c) The illustrations highlight the importance of carefully controlling the structure and properties of porous carbon by chemical vapor deposition. Reproduced with permission.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit126"><sup><span class="sup_ref">126</span></sup></a> Copyright 2003, Elesiver. (d) A suggested mechanism for prelithiation involving LiBpTHF in HC. Reproduced with permission.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit128"><sup><span class="sup_ref">128</span></sup></a> Copyright 2020, American Chemical Society. (e) The diagram depicts the chemical prelithiation reaction of HC in a Li-Naph/DME solution and the subsequent formation of SEI on the pHC anode during cycling in a Li-ion battery electrolyte. (f) The SEI of pre-lithiated HC observed by TEM. (g) The SEI of pristine HC observed by TEM. (e)(g) Reproduced with permission.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit127"><sup><span class="sup_ref">127</span></sup></a> Copyright 2020, Wiley-VCH Verlag GmbH &amp; Co. KGaA, Weinheim.</span></span></td><td class="pushTitleLeft"> </td></tr></tbody></table></div>
      
      
        
        <h3 id="sect765"><span class="b_heading">4.2 Surface optimization in HC-SIBs and HC-LIBs</span></h3>
        <span>The irreversibility of HC is closely related to surface chemistry, which can vary greatly when exposed to different atmospheres.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit44"><sup><span class="sup_ref">44</span></sup></a> Different surface structures can lead to the formation of very different SEI, so modifying their type to trigger the formation of an ideal SEI is encouraging. Varying the type of functional group to more reversible sites can be achieved through various methods such as pre-oxidation, acid treatment, and post-reduction and doping. In the study by Wang <span class="italic">et al.</span>,<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit130"><sup><span class="sup_ref">130</span></sup></a> they employed <span class="italic">in situ</span> engineering techniques by mixing of cyclohexane molecules with Ar during the carbonization. This method helped to reduce the residual oxygen and defects (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig12">Fig. 12a</a>), resulting in an improvement of the ICE from 70% to 85% (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig12">Fig. 12b</a>). However, researchers found that heteroatoms and defects could be a double-edged sword. While the right amount of oxygen functional groups and defects is beneficial for Na-ion adsorption, an excess can led to stronger trapping, which cannot be extracted at the cut-off voltage of 2 V. When they introduced more oxygen functional groups and defects by immersing the C-HC in acid, the ICE decreased to 30%. The group of Wang has developed a reduction strategy to modify the surface of HC in an H<small><sub>2</sub></small>/Ar atmosphere,<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit59"><sup><span class="sup_ref">59</span></sup></a> as shown in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig12">Fig. 12c</a>. This process reduces the number of defects, such as oxygen-containing functional groups and dangling bonds, resulting in an initial reversible capacity of approximately 400 mA h g<small><sup>1</sup></small> with an ICE of 79%. These results demonstrate that surface modification is an effective strategy for improving the ICE. However, the initial loss of Na-ions involved in the formation of SEI can still occur, which can be reduced by coating the material. The coating layer helps to homogenize the surface chemistry by covering some irreversible defect sites and reducing the number of electrolyte decomposition sites,<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit131"><sup><span class="sup_ref">131133</span></sup></a> which involves mixing the coated material with the precursor<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit132"><sup><span class="sup_ref">132</span></sup></a> and then annealing or coating by the pyrolysis of toluene during carbonization.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit133"><sup><span class="sup_ref">133</span></sup></a> One effective approach involves using an amphiphilic carbonaceous material mixed with porous carbon, resulting in a coated sample called COAT-10-1400, as shown in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig12">Fig. 12d</a>. This coating modification preserves the internal porous structure while greatly reducing the SSA.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit132"><sup><span class="sup_ref">132</span></sup></a> The activated low-voltage storage sites can be tailored by modifying the surface chemistry. As a result, the COAT-10-1400 sample exhibits an extremely high plateau capacity of 293 mA h g<small><sup>1</sup></small> without loss of sloping capacity, and a reversible capacity of 391 mA h g<small><sup>1</sup></small> with a high ICE of 80% (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig12">Fig. 12e</a>). In research carried out by Chou <span class="italic">et al.</span>,<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit131"><sup><span class="sup_ref">131</span></sup></a> a paper towel was impregnated with coal tar pitch, and the coal tar pitch forms a soft carbon coating on the HC surface during subsequent carbonization. An ultra-high ICE of 94.5% was obtained.</span>
        <br><div class="image_table"><table><tbody><tr><td colspan="3" class="imgHolder" id="imgfig12"><a href="/image/article/2023/EE/d3ee02202a/d3ee02202a-f12_hi-res.gif" title="Select to open image in new window" onclick="open(this.href, &quot;_blank&quot;, &quot;toolbar=1,scrollbars=yes,resizable=1&quot;); return false;"><img alt="image file: d3ee02202a-f12.tif" src="/image/article/2023/EE/d3ee02202a/d3ee02202a-f12.gif"></a></td></tr><tr><td class="pushTitleRight"> </td><td class="image_title"><b>Fig. 12 </b> <span id="fig12"><span class="graphic_title">Strategies to improve ICE by modifying surface chemistry. (a) The diagram of defect repair and heteroatom functional group reduction. (b) The initial discharge-charge curves of P-HC, N-HC, and C-HC at a current density of 20 mA g<small><sup>1</sup></small>. (a) and (b) Reproduced with permission.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit130"><sup><span class="sup_ref">130</span></sup></a> Copyright 2019, Elesiver. (c) A reduction mechanism representation of the H<small><sub>2</sub></small>/Ar atmosphere during the pyrolysis process. Reproduced with permission.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit134"><sup><span class="sup_ref">134</span></sup></a> Copyright 2018, Elesiver. (d) A diagrammatic representation of the approach towards coating which aims to decrease the contact area while preserving the internal porous framework. (e) The initial discharge/charge curves at a current rate of 0.1C. (d) and (e) Reproduced with permission.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit132"><sup><span class="sup_ref">132</span></sup></a> Copyright 2018, Elesiver.</span></span></td><td class="pushTitleLeft"> </td></tr></tbody></table></div>
        <p class="otherpara">The strategy of doping is widely used in HC-LIBs.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit102"><sup><span class="sup_ref">102,103</span></sup></a> Through a research study, Yang <span class="italic">et al.</span><a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit135"><sup><span class="sup_ref">135</span></sup></a> found that doped P atoms easily combine with O atoms to form PO bonds at the edge of the carbon, facilitating the formation of a thin and dense SEI, allowing only Li-ions to shuttle and alleviating the extra-decomposition of electrolyte (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig13">Fig. 13a</a>). An increase in ICE from 36.9% to 70.9% is observed (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig13">Fig. 13b</a>). Similarly, S-doped porous carbon shows a better performance compared to the undoped sample, with a reversible capacity of 1781 mA h g<small><sup>1</sup></small> and an increase in ICE from 39.6% to 81%.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit136"><sup><span class="sup_ref">136</span></sup></a> They proposed a mechanism which is shown in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig13">Fig. 13c</a>. The doping of S is mostly directly bound to the carbon matrix to form CS bonds, thus removing irreversible oxygen functional groups as well as some active carbon segments. In another doping work, the Cl doping enhances the intensity of all peaks in soft X-ray absorption near-edge structure (XANES) spectroscopy, which is further improved by theoretical calculation.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit137"><sup><span class="sup_ref">137</span></sup></a> It gains electrons from the carbon matrix more easily than the solvent molecules due to its stronger electronegativity (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig13">Fig. 13d</a>), making it more difficult for the solvent molecules to be reduced to form SEI. As a result, the initial reversible capacity increased by almost 15% (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig13">Fig. 13e</a>). Sulfuric acid treatment had little effect on the microstructural changes but affected the surface functional groups, as shown in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig13">Fig. 13f</a>. The acid treatment resulted in a 20% increase in the reversible capacity, which was attributed to the weak basicity of the newly introduced conjugate bases of the SO<small><sub>3</sub></small>H and SO<small><sub>4</sub></small>H.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit138"><sup><span class="sup_ref">138</span></sup></a> In addition, pre-oxidation at an appropriate low temperature is another effective surface modify strategy to obtain the desired functional groups and prevent over-graphitization.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit139"><sup><span class="sup_ref">139</span></sup></a> The authors believe that pre-oxidation can improve the cross-linking of the precursor and make the carbon matrix remain stable in the subsequent carbonization at high temperature. Surface engineering by exposure to H<small><sub>2</sub></small>O vapor is used to not only introduce carbonyl C<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_e001.gif" alt="[double bond, length as m-dash]">O and OC<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_e001.gif" alt="[double bond, length as m-dash]">O.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit140"><sup><span class="sup_ref">140</span></sup></a> On the one hand, it adds reversible sites for Li-ions in the sloping region, which is also beneficial for rate performance, and on the other hand, it induces the formation of an appropriate SEI, which has a smaller <span class="italic">R</span><small><sub>sei</sub></small>. In another similar work, Fujimoto <span class="italic">et al.</span><a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit113"><sup><span class="sup_ref">113</span></sup></a> obtained a better capacity through mild modification by oxidation in air, which only changes the surface structure without destroying the bulk structure. The optimized structure has a narrowed pore-entrance diameter, which allows Li-ions to enter while excluding electrolyte solvent molecules, then the irreversible capacity is reduced. However, in the case of strong oxidation with P<small><sub>2</sub></small>O<small><sub>5</sub></small>, the pore structure is disrupted, allowing the entry of ions other than Li-ions, resulting in a higher irreversible capacity. So, it is essential to ensure that the modification method would not disrupt the pore structure. Overall, careful selection of the modification method is crucial to achieve the desired reversible capacity and improve the rate performance. It is well-know that raising the HTT results in the elimination of some irreversible storage sites for Li-ions, but also leads to the loss of reversible storage sites.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit82"><sup><span class="sup_ref">82</span></sup></a> To overcome this issue, post-reduction techniques are implemented that involve removing functional groups like CO and quinone, which can impede the reversibility of Li-ion storage.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit141"><sup><span class="sup_ref">141,142</span></sup></a> In a post-thermal treatment performed in an H<small><sub>2</sub></small>/Ar atmosphere, C<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_e001.gif" alt="[double bond, length as m-dash]">O functionality, which is conducive to the desorption of Li-ions, is optimized. At 400 C, the optimal temperature for reversible desorption, a high content of C<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_e001.gif" alt="[double bond, length as m-dash]">O leads to superior performance, as shown by a reversible capacity of 579 mA h g<small><sup>1</sup></small> and an improved ICE from 39.7% to 51.9%.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit142"><sup><span class="sup_ref">142</span></sup></a></p>
        <br><div class="image_table"><table><tbody><tr><td colspan="3" class="imgHolder" id="imgfig13"><a href="/image/article/2023/EE/d3ee02202a/d3ee02202a-f13_hi-res.gif" title="Select to open image in new window" onclick="open(this.href, &quot;_blank&quot;, &quot;toolbar=1,scrollbars=yes,resizable=1&quot;); return false;"><img alt="image file: d3ee02202a-f13.tif" src="/image/article/2023/EE/d3ee02202a/d3ee02202a-f13.gif"></a></td></tr><tr><td class="pushTitleRight"> </td><td class="image_title"><b>Fig. 13 </b> <span id="fig13"><span class="graphic_title">(a) Demonstration of varying SEI structures observed on the P-doped carbon (PO/C) and the pure carbon (PC-800). (b) The initial 2 cycle discharge/charge curves of P/C and C-800. (a) and (b) Reproduced with permission.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit135"><sup><span class="sup_ref">135</span></sup></a> Copyright 2018, American Chemical Society. (c) A proposed mechanism to improve the ICE through the incorporation of S dopants compared with the undoped carbon. Reproduced with permission.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit136"><sup><span class="sup_ref">136</span></sup></a> Copyright 2014, American Chemical Society. (d) The diagram illustrates that Cl doping enables easier acquisition of electrons compared to electrolyte-molecule, thus mitigating the irreversible capacity resulting from the reduction of electrolyte solvent molecules. (e) Initial dischargecharge curves of Cl-HC and pure HC. (d) and (e) Reproduced with permission.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit137"><sup><span class="sup_ref">137</span></sup></a> Copyright 2021, Elsevier. (f) Suggested reactions for sulfonation of carbon based on PAN, through treatment with sulfuric acid. Reproduced with permission.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit138"><sup><span class="sup_ref">138</span></sup></a> Copyright 2005, Elesiver.</span></span></td><td class="pushTitleLeft"> </td></tr></tbody></table></div>
      
      
        
        <h3 id="sect832"><span class="b_heading">4.3 Composite material and doping strategies in HC-SIBs and HC-LIBs</span></h3>
        <span>Doping is a popular method to enhance HC materials' electronic conductivity and <span class="italic">R</span><small><sub>ct</sub></small>.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit143"><sup><span class="sup_ref">143</span></sup></a> Elements that are more electronegative than carbon, create regions with an electron-rich environment near doping sites, leading to enhanced electrochemical performance. Moreover, doping modification increases SSA and enhances the Na-ions binding affinity, improving the electrode's capacity to preserve charge in high-rate applications. Though it contributes to significant improvements in rate and cyclability, the doping strategy has limitations including a low ICE, and a substantial dilution of cathode material and electrolyte leads to low ICE that rarely surpasses 50%.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit25"><sup><span class="sup_ref">25,80,143147</span></sup></a> Hence, doping strategies are unlikely to play an essential role in commercializing HC in SIBs any time soon.</span>
        <p class="otherpara">While for HC in LIBs, HC-LIBs have a higher reversible capacity than graphite-based LIBs, but their energy density is limited due to a low ICE and high electrode potential with voltage hysteresis. To address these challenges, researchers are exploring the use of composite anodes made by combining HC with other materials,<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit125"><sup><span class="sup_ref">125,148151</span></sup></a> such as graphite/HC, nano-Sn/HC, and graphene oxide/HC. Of these, a homogeneous mixture of graphite and HC has shown great potential (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig14">Fig. 14a</a>). By adjusting the blend ratio, an optimum energy density of up to 180 W h kg<small><sup>1</sup></small> can be achieved by Dasgupta and his colleagues,<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit148"><sup><span class="sup_ref">148</span></sup></a> which is similar to existing commercial graphite-based LIBs (200 W h kg<small><sup>1</sup></small>). They credit the enhanced performance to the subsequent factors: Li-ions are first inserted into the HC due to its relative positive potential compared to graphite. And the disordered structure with sufficient space of HC prevents lithium overload and reduces the current load to the graphite. The graphite, in turn, compensates for the additional capacity at a low voltage. As a result, the graphite/HC composite anode has improved fast charge performance, with 87% specific energy retention after 500 cycles at 4C compared to 61% for graphite-based LIBs. Wang <span class="italic">et al.</span> also presented a composite anode made of nano-Sn with HC in a separate study.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit125"><sup><span class="sup_ref">125</span></sup></a> The anode demonstrated an impressive ICE of 76% when the cut-off charge voltage set at 2.5 V. Additionally, by increasing the cut-off charge voltage to 3 V, the anode displayed an additional capacity of approximately 90 mA h g<small><sup>1</sup></small> at 2.8 V. The researchers attributed this phenomenon to the nano-Sn, which facilitated the decomposition of the SEI, thereby releasing Li-ions from ROCO<small><sub>2</sub></small>Li. In addition, doping has proved to be an effective solution improving the conductivity of HC, while leveraging ion catalysis to enhance its performance without any side effects.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit152"><sup><span class="sup_ref">152</span></sup></a> In a study by the group of John B. Goodenough,<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit73"><sup><span class="sup_ref">73</span></sup></a> a chelation effect between phenol and Fe<small><sup>3+</sup></small> was utilized to create a phenol-formaldehyde resin-based hard-carbon aerogel with a three-dimensional carbon network embedded with ordered microstructures of expanded nanographites and carbon micropores (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig14">Fig. 14b</a>). The chemical modification strategy demonstrates efficient catalytic graphitization due to the homodispersion of Fe<small><sup>3+</sup></small> in the polymer segments of the precursor, allowing for free rearrangement of graphene sheets into extended nanographite and carbon micropores. Under the influence of Fe<small><sup>3+</sup></small> catalysis, the curved carbon layer undergoes a process of diminishing curvature, resulting in alignment of the graphene with less curvature in a parallel configuration. This enhanced structural order improves the ordering of the graphene material, facilitates diffusion, and leads to an escalated rate performance. Interestingly, in the work of Fan <span class="italic">et al.</span>,<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit153"><sup><span class="sup_ref">153</span></sup></a> an emerging reversible capacity of 145.8 mA h g<small><sup>1</sup></small> at the plateau region was achieved by boric acid-catalyzed annealing at 800 C (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig14">Fig. 14c</a>). The results of XPS, Raman, and HRTEM show a more orderly structure with larger graphite-like crystals, which facilitates one part of the emerging capacity. They attribute the other emerging capacity to the change in pore size, but we think it may be the optimized pore-entrance diameter, which screens out electrolyte-molecular to decomposition in the pore. In another work, Xiong <span class="italic">et al.</span> present a novel carbon framework anode that exhibits ultrafast rate and cycling stability attributable to nitrogen and phosphorus doping.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit154"><sup><span class="sup_ref">154</span></sup></a> The electrode comprises a 3D framework fabricated from 2D heteroatom-doped graphene layers (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig14">Fig. 14d</a>), which facilitates fast kinetics in charge storage and transport. The nanostructured 3D framework, coupled with chemical doping, yields a high reversible capacity of 946 mA h g<small><sup>1</sup></small> at 0.5 A g<small><sup>1</sup></small>, while maintaining an excellent rate capability even up to 10 A g<small><sup>1</sup></small>. Furthermore, the electrode demonstrates remarkable cycling stability at 2 A g<small><sup>1</sup></small> over 2000 cycles. The authors assert that the electrode's superior Li<small><sup>+</sup></small> storage performance, arise from the 3D porous nanostructured carbon framework and the topological defects, which engender multidimensional interconnected pathways for Li-ion diffusion, and create more active sites for Li<small><sup>+</sup></small> storage, respectively. In a recent investigation, Lin produced P-doped HC (PHC-700) through a combination of phosphoric acid and epoxy resin, followed by annealing at 700 C.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit155"><sup><span class="sup_ref">155</span></sup></a> The resulting PHC-700 demonstrated a superior reversible capacity of 1294.8 mA h g<small><sup>1</sup></small> and improved ICE of 60.4% compared to HC-700, which displayed a reversible capacity of 542.8 mA h g<small><sup>1</sup></small> and an ICE of 43.8%. To elucidate the underlying mechanism, <span class="italic">in situ</span> Raman and <span class="italic">in situ</span> static solid-state NMR techniques were utilized. A new peak at 200 to 500 ppm of <span class="italic">in situ</span> static solid-state NMR was observed (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig14">Fig. 14e</a>), which corresponds to the Li<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>PC<small><sub><span class="italic">y</span></sub></small> and suggests the introduction of P and additional storage sites. The sharp signal at <span class="italic"></span>  0 ppm represented the PO bond, while the broad signal between <span class="italic"></span>  200 and 100 ppm represented the PC bond (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig14">Fig. 14f</a>). There are no significant changes in the PO signal, while the PC bond signal broadened and then recovered its initial width during discharge/charge, indicating a reversible redox reaction between Li-ions and the PC bond. The results of <span class="italic">in situ</span> Raman showed that the D and G bands weakened during discharge and were restored during charge in both samples (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig14">Fig. 14g and f</a>), but HC-700 exhibited a lower degree of reversible recovery (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig14">Fig. 14g</a>), indicating more-trapped Li-ions and subsequent structural disruption.</p>
        <br><div class="image_table"><table><tbody><tr><td colspan="3" class="imgHolder" id="imgfig14"><a href="/image/article/2023/EE/d3ee02202a/d3ee02202a-f14_hi-res.gif" title="Select to open image in new window" onclick="open(this.href, &quot;_blank&quot;, &quot;toolbar=1,scrollbars=yes,resizable=1&quot;); return false;"><img alt="image file: d3ee02202a-f14.tif" src="/image/article/2023/EE/d3ee02202a/d3ee02202a-f14.gif"></a></td></tr><tr><td class="pushTitleRight"> </td><td class="image_title"><b>Fig. 14 </b> <span id="fig14"><span class="graphic_title">(a) The diagram displays the superiority of HC/graphite composite materials. Reproduced with permission.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit148"><sup><span class="sup_ref">148</span></sup></a> Copyright 2020, Wiley-VCH GmbH. (b) A proposed mechanism for lithium storage in the ordered expanded nanographite domain of mPFR-HCA (Fe<small><sup>3+</sup></small> catalyzed), as well as in the turbostratic microarchitecture of PFR-HCA (pristine). Reproduced with permission.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit73"><sup><span class="sup_ref">73</span></sup></a> Copyright 2016, American Chemical Society. (c) The galvanostatic charge voltage profiles of pure HC and boric acid-catalyzed HC. Reproduced with permission.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit153"><sup><span class="sup_ref">153</span></sup></a> Copyright 2019, Wiley-VCH Verlag GmbH &amp; Co. KGaA, Weinheim. (d) The NP-C synthesis was carried out using two main components: M1, which comprised phytic acid, and M2, which consisted of melamine. Reproduced with permission.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit154"><sup><span class="sup_ref">154</span></sup></a> Copyright 2018, American Chemical Society. (e) The first discharge/charge process of PHC-700 was characterized through <span class="italic">in situ</span><small><sup>31</sup></small>P static solid-state NMR spectra. (f) Enlarged spectra of that shown in (e) were also analyzed. <span class="italic">In situ</span> Raman spectroscopy to investigate the first discharge/charge process of (g) HC-700 and (h) PHC-700. (e)(h) Reproduced with permission.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit155"><sup><span class="sup_ref">155</span></sup></a> Copyright 2019, Wiley-VCH Verlag GmbH &amp; Co. KGaA, Weinheim.</span></span></td><td class="pushTitleLeft"> </td></tr></tbody></table></div>
      
      
        
        <h3 id="sect897"><span class="b_heading">4.4 Tailoring of pore structure in HC-SIBs</span></h3>
        <span>The closure of pores in non-oriented graphene sheets is crucial for increasing the plateau capacity of HC-SIBs,<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit81"><sup><span class="sup_ref">81</span></sup></a> and HTT is a key factor in regulating pore structure.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit67"><sup><span class="sup_ref">67,90,156158</span></sup></a> However, excessive heating may lead to over-graphitization that will cause poor cycling and rate performance. To mitigate this, Titirici <span class="italic">et al.</span> developed a hard/soft composite material at 1000 C,<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit159"><sup><span class="sup_ref">159</span></sup></a> where the introduced soft carbon covers surface entrances or fills open pores, effectively reducing the irreversible capacity caused by electrolyte decomposition. In addition, pore-forming agents have been employed. A solvothermal process using ethanol can generate pores before carbonization (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig15">Fig. 15a</a>),<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit158"><sup><span class="sup_ref">158</span></sup></a> which act as Na-ion filling sites, and through precise adjustments of their respective compositions during solvothermal synthesis, followed by carbonization, it is feasible to synthesis HC with optimal closed-pore microstructures, as opposed to directly carbonized samples. Thus, an excellent performance of 410 mA h g<small><sup>1</sup></small> and an ICE of 84% was acquired (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig15">Fig. 15b</a>). Tailoring the diameter of pore-entrance is another encouraging strategy. Yang <span class="italic">et al.</span> conducted a study using sieving carbon to produce a large capacity of HC.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit84"><sup><span class="sup_ref">84</span></sup></a> They optimized the porous structure by regulating the pore-entrance diameter (&lt;0.4 nm) and pore body diameter (&lt;2 nm). A narrowed pore-entrance prevents the decomposition of electrolyte within the nanopores, leading to the clustering of sodium and the resulting creation of a plateau capacity, which also prevents the formation of an unwanted SEI within them, while the electrochemically responsive pore surface is safeguarded, allowing for the development of sodium clusters (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig15">Fig. 15c</a>). They achieved an unprecedented plateau capacity of 400 mA h g<small><sup>1</sup></small> (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig15">Fig. 15d</a>). Additionally, the conversion from an open-pore dominated structure to a closed-pore dominated structure is encouraging. Direct carbonization of anthracite leads to a small interlayer distance and low pore volume (DC1300). While DC1300 boasts an initial reversible capacity of 221.7 mA h g<small><sup>1</sup></small> but with a low ICE of only 78.6%, which is contributed to open-pore dominated structures providing storage sites for Na-ions but also sites for electrolyte decomposition, which can create excessive SEI film formation during the initial process. To solve this problem, KOH-assisted chemical activation prior to carbonization (AC700) can convert open pores into closed pores,<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit156"><sup><span class="sup_ref">156</span></sup></a> producing predominantly closed pore HC (CAC1300) with a larger interlayer spacing and increased pore volume (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig15">Fig. 15e</a>). As a result, CAC1300 delivers a reversible capacity of 308.4 mA h g<small><sup>1</sup></small> with a high ICE of 82.3% (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig15">Fig. 15f</a>). These findings indicate that meticulous management of a detailed pore-structure is essential for attaining maximum capacity, and that fine-tuning the pore-entrance can significantly enhance its performance.</span>
        <br><div class="image_table"><table><tbody><tr><td colspan="3" class="imgHolder" id="imgfig15"><a href="/image/article/2023/EE/d3ee02202a/d3ee02202a-f15_hi-res.gif" title="Select to open image in new window" onclick="open(this.href, &quot;_blank&quot;, &quot;toolbar=1,scrollbars=yes,resizable=1&quot;); return false;"><img alt="image file: d3ee02202a-f15.tif" src="/image/article/2023/EE/d3ee02202a/d3ee02202a-f15.gif"></a></td></tr><tr><td class="pushTitleRight"> </td><td class="image_title"><b>Fig. 15 </b> <span id="fig15"><span class="graphic_title">Strategies to improve ICE in HC-SIBs by tailoring pore-structure. (a) The structural dissimilarity existing between the directly carbonized sample and the one that employs EtOH as the pore-forming agent. (b) Initial discharge/charge curves of samples with different proportions of EtOH. (a) and (b) Reproduced with permission.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit158"><sup><span class="sup_ref">158</span></sup></a> Copyright 2019, American Chemical Society. (c) A diagram illustration of tightening pore-entrance diameter. (d) Initial discharge/charge curves of different SCs at a current density of 50 mA g<small><sup>1</sup></small>, porous carbon serves as the starting material for both SC-X and SC-M. The former is named with the abbreviation SC-X, with the letter X following a numerical sequence indicating varying specific surface areas. The larger the X value, the greater the specific surface area. SC-M is created from PC-M. (c) and (d) Reproduced with permission.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit84"><sup><span class="sup_ref">84</span></sup></a> Copyright 2022,Oxford academic. (e) Differences in pore structures produced by different methods. The AC700 sample was created by activating charcoal with KOH at a temperature of 700 C. The DC1300 sample was obtained by directly carbonizing anthracite. Lastly, the CAC1300 sample was produced by further carbonizing the AC700 sample at a temperature of 1300 C. (f) Initial discharge/charge curves of AC700, DC1300 and DC1300. (e-f) Reproduced with permission.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit156"><sup><span class="sup_ref">156</span></sup></a> Copyright 2022, Wiley-VCH GmbH.</span></span></td><td class="pushTitleLeft"> </td></tr></tbody></table></div>
      
      
        
        <h3 id="sect925"><span class="b_heading">4.5 Structure design of electrode material in HC-SIBs</span></h3>
        <span>Modulating the structure of HC is an effective strategy for improving its rate and cycle performance. For optimal performance in terms of reversible capacity, ICE, rate performance, lifespan, and a slope-dominated HC with acceptable ICE is desirable. This was achieved by the group of Hu,<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit160"><sup><span class="sup_ref">160</span></sup></a> who synthesised HC using pitch as a precursor at a low temperature of 800 C. The HC retained a large number of defects and oxygen functional groups that provided sufficient adsorption sites. This resulted in an amorphous structure with a lower diffusion barrier for Na-ions compared with the graphitic region, which is favorable for rate performance. The HC delivered 124 mA h g<small><sup>1</sup></small> at 12C and 236 mA h g<small><sup>1</sup></small> at 0.15C. The group also designed unique multi-shell hollow carbon (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig16">Fig. 16a</a>).<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit161"><sup><span class="sup_ref">161</span></sup></a> The creation of the shell structure reduced the <span class="italic">R</span><small><sub>ct</sub></small>, resulting in improved kinetics thus showing a good rate performance (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig16">Fig. 16b</a>). The positive correlation between the number of shells and the plateau-capacity suggests that the introduction of shells provides additional adsorption-filling sites. The improved cycle life (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig16">Fig. 16c</a>) is attributed to the flexibility of the multi-shell hollow carbon, which can alleviate the structural variation caused by Na-ions insertion/extraction. However, the plateau-dominated carbon achieved inferior performance compared to slope-dominated carbon, delivering only 200 mA h g<small><sup>1</sup></small> at 2C and 360 mA h g<small><sup>1</sup></small> at 0.1C. In addition, the poor electron conductivity of the non-graphitizable carbon region in HC is a reason for the poor rate performance. By artificially introducing some other materials with higher conductivity such as graphene, it has been shown to be valid to increase the rate performance and cycle life. A sandwich-like hierarchical porous carbon/graphene composite is shown in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig16">Fig. 16d</a>. On the one hand, the graphene features excellent electronic conductivity to ensure fast transport of Na-ions and electrons, on the other hand, the graphene on both sides of the HC also stabilizes structural changes due to the insertion/extraction of Na-ions,<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit162"><sup><span class="sup_ref">162</span></sup></a> resulting in good cycling performance with a retention capacity of 250 mA h g<small><sup>1</sup></small> after 1000 cycles at 1 A g<small><sup>1</sup></small> (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig16">Fig. 16e</a>).</span>
        <br><div class="image_table"><table><tbody><tr><td colspan="3" class="imgHolder" id="imgfig16"><a href="/image/article/2023/EE/d3ee02202a/d3ee02202a-f16_hi-res.gif" title="Select to open image in new window" onclick="open(this.href, &quot;_blank&quot;, &quot;toolbar=1,scrollbars=yes,resizable=1&quot;); return false;"><img alt="image file: d3ee02202a-f16.tif" src="/image/article/2023/EE/d3ee02202a/d3ee02202a-f16.gif"></a></td></tr><tr><td class="pushTitleRight"> </td><td class="image_title"><b>Fig. 16 </b> <span id="fig16"><span class="graphic_title">(a) Schematic diagram of the synthetic route of MS-HCNs, with M representing the number of shells. (b) The rate performance. (c) Cycle performance of 4S-HCN and the controlled sample solid-CN at 30 mA g<small><sup>1</sup></small>. (a)(c) Reproduced with permission.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit161"><sup><span class="sup_ref">161</span></sup></a> Copyright 2018, Wiley-VCH Verlag GmbH &amp; Co. KGaA, Weinheim. (d) Composite of HC and graphene, where the graphene acts as an additional channel for electron transport. (d) Cycling performance of the HC/graphene composite material under 1 A g<small><sup>1</sup></small>. (d) and (e) Reproduced with permission.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit162"><sup><span class="sup_ref">162</span></sup></a> Copyright 2014, Wiley-VCH Verlag GmbH &amp; Co. KGaA, Weinheim.</span></span></td><td class="pushTitleLeft"> </td></tr></tbody></table></div>
      
      
        
        <h3 id="sect951"><span class="b_heading">4.6 Optimizing electrolyte in HC-SIBs</span></h3>
        <span>Electrolyte optimization is a crucial strategy for improving the rate and cycling performance of HC-SIBs.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit35"><sup><span class="sup_ref">35,163166</span></sup></a> Recent work by the group of Lu has shown that the sluggish kinetics of Na-ions across the SEI can be attributed to the desolvation process. To address this issue, they developed a desolvation strategy called step-by-step in which 3A zeolite molecular sieves larger than HC-electrode are first attached to HC-electrode,<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit163"><sup><span class="sup_ref">163</span></sup></a> and then the electrolyte is added, thereby avoiding direct contact between the electrolyte and HC. This approach leads to a thin and inorganic-dominated SEI with a lower activation energy barrier (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig17">Fig. 17a and b</a>), which greatly improves the rate performance (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig17">Fig. 17c</a>). When the current density varies from 0.05 to 2 A g<small><sup>1</sup></small>, the capacity loss is only 17.7%, and it still provides 224 mA h g<small><sup>1</sup></small> at 5 A g<small><sup>1</sup></small>. Moreover, the capacity retention is up to 94.1% after 4000 cycles at 0.5 A g<small><sup>1</sup></small>. In fact, the problem of unstable SEI film can be optimized by introducing additives, and the researchers adjusted the amount of VC additive to form a thin and dense SEI film.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit164"><sup><span class="sup_ref">164</span></sup></a> When 0.5% VC was added, a structurally and chemically stable, mechanically durable SEI film was formed (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig17">Fig. 17d</a>) compared to that without VC (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig17">Fig. 17e</a>). This resulted in a capacity retention of 95.6% after 2000 cycles at 1 A g<small><sup>1</sup></small>, compared to only 72.3% without VC. Interestingly, HC electrodes performed better in TEGDME than in carbonate at almost all current densities (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig17">Fig. 17g</a>), and this difference is primarily determined by the property of the SEI.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit120"><sup><span class="sup_ref">120</span></sup></a> Specifically, the SEI in TEGDME is thinner than in carbonate, which leads to easier Na-ions transfer kinetics. <span class="italic">In situ</span> EIS measurements showed that passing ions through TEGDME-based HC is easier due to the smaller <span class="italic">R</span><small><sub>ct</sub></small> and <span class="italic">R</span><small><sub>sei</sub></small>. In addition, <span class="italic">R</span><small><sub>sei</sub></small> decreases and the SEI becomes more conductive when Na-ions intercalation is initiated. The SEI in TEGDME forms quickly and stabilizes around 0.5 V, while it is stable up to 0.2 V in carbonate. As a result, the Na-ion intercalation process only needs to overcome the interfacial barrier between the SEI and the HC, while it must overcome both the interfacial barrier and SEI barrier in carbonated-based SEI. Huilin Pan and colleagues have provided new insights into the factors affecting the rate and cycle performance of SEI films.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit35"><sup><span class="sup_ref">35</span></sup></a> They found that it is not the different composition of SEI, but rather the different nanoscale structure of SEI that plays a crucial role in battery performance. When carbonate solvents are used, they tend to form a loose SEI film consisting of an organicinorganic mixture, which is characterized by a disordered arrangement of different component structures. This leads to the formation of a thick and rough SEI film, which increases the <span class="italic">R</span><small><sub>sei</sub></small> and is prone to collapse during cycling. On the other hand, when the solvent-salt solvates over time, they form a dense and layer-by-layer SEI film that is characterized by uniformity. This nano-engineered SEI film enhances ion transport and has good stability during cycling. <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig17">Fig. 17f</a> illustrates the differences in SEI formation mechanisms.</span>
        <br><div class="image_table"><table><tbody><tr><td colspan="3" class="imgHolder" id="imgfig17"><a href="/image/article/2023/EE/d3ee02202a/d3ee02202a-f17_hi-res.gif" title="Select to open image in new window" onclick="open(this.href, &quot;_blank&quot;, &quot;toolbar=1,scrollbars=yes,resizable=1&quot;); return false;"><img alt="image file: d3ee02202a-f17.tif" src="/image/article/2023/EE/d3ee02202a/d3ee02202a-f17.gif"></a></td></tr><tr><td class="pushTitleRight"> </td><td class="image_title"><b>Fig. 17 </b> <span id="fig17"><span class="graphic_title">(a) The kinetics of desolvation and Na<small><sup>+</sup></small> transport <span class="italic">via</span> the SEI in pristine ether electrolytes and (b) predesolvated electrolytes were contrasted. (c) The rate performance at different current densities was evaluated in EC/DEC electrolytes and predesolvated electrolytes. (a)(c) Reproduced with permission.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit163"><sup><span class="sup_ref">163</span></sup></a> Copyright 2022, Ziyang Lua. Published by PNAS. (d) Illustration of the additive VC helping to form a stable SEI during cycle. (e) Unstable SEI during cycling without the addition of VC. (d)(e) Reproduced with permission.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit164"><sup><span class="sup_ref">164</span></sup></a> Copyright 2020, Elesiver. (f) An illustration of the way in which the different components are arranged in relation to each other is an even greater reason for the variability in performance of the different SEI films. (g) Rate performance of HC electrodes in carbonate electrolytes and TEGDME. (f)(g) Reproduced with permission.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit35"><sup><span class="sup_ref">35</span></sup></a> Copyright 2021, Wiley-VCH GmbH.</span></span></td><td class="pushTitleLeft"> </td></tr></tbody></table></div>
      
    
    
      
      <h2 id="sect987"><span class="a_heading">5 State of the art and future potential</span></h2>
      
        
        <h3 id="sect990"><span class="b_heading">5.1 Hard carbon in lithium-ion batteries</span></h3>
        <span>Graphite-based LIBs have been the standard anode material for commercial LIBs over the past 30 years, benefiting from their low operating potential, high energy density, and long cycle life. However, they suffer from several challenges (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig18">Fig. 18</a>), including safety issues due to lithium plating on graphite, limited theoretical capacity, poor fast-charging performance, and poor performance at low temperatures. These challenges limit their further development, particularly in applications such as new energy vehicles and energy storage. The search for new anode materials has become the key to the success of next-generation LIBs. HC anode has been identified as a promising alternative to graphite due to its advantages in capacity, cycle life, and performance at low temperatures and more safety.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit167"><sup><span class="sup_ref">167,168</span></sup></a> HC-LIBs are expected to make breakthroughs in higher energy/power vehicles, start/stop batteries, low-temperature applications, and the stationary energy storage industry. Graphite-based LIBs are inadequate under extreme conditions, such as space and cold-district at high altitude, where they experience significantly reduced power and energy density below 0 C,<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit169"><sup><span class="sup_ref">169,170</span></sup></a> with a capacity loss of approximately 88% at 20 C compared to room temperature.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit171"><sup><span class="sup_ref">171</span></sup></a> This drawback at low temperature is due to low electrolyte conductivity and increased <span class="italic">R</span><small><sub>ct</sub></small> and <span class="italic">R</span><small><sub>sei</sub></small>. The increased resistance increases the potential for dendrite formation, which can lead to explosions and short circuits.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit172"><sup><span class="sup_ref">172</span></sup></a> The increased <span class="italic">R</span><small><sub>ct</sub></small> at low temperatures can be mitigated using additives, but it has less effect on <span class="italic">R</span><small><sub>sei</sub></small>.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit173"><sup><span class="sup_ref">173</span></sup></a> Propylene carbonate (PC)-based electrolytes have high ionic mobility even in a low-temperature environment, making them advantageous for improving low-temperature performance. However, the intercalation of co-solvent molecules destroys the structure of graphite, while the amorphous structure of HC accommodates the insertion of co-solvent molecules. So, replacing graphite anodes with HC in LIBs is promising for improving low-temperature performance. Wang <span class="italic">et al.</span><a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit174"><sup><span class="sup_ref">174</span></sup></a> assembled a full-cell based on a prelithiated HC/Li<small><sub>2</sub></small>V<small><sub>2</sub></small>PO<small><sub>4</sub></small>, which provided an energy density of 208.3 W h kg<small><sup>1</sup></small>, close to graphite-based LIBs, and an excellent power energy of 8291 W kg<small><sup>1</sup></small>. When applied at 40 C at a rate of 0.25C, 67% of the capacity can still be achieved compared with that at room temperature. HC-LIBs also have potential applications in start/stop batteries. Start/stop batteries are a system in newer car models that automatically start and stop the engine to reduce fuel consumption. They require high charge/discharge power at high rates, and HC-LIBs are more suitable for start-stop batteries than lead-acid batteries due to their lighter mass, smaller volume, lower internal resistance, and longer cycle life. HC-LIBs are promising for use in EVs and HEVs due to their high energy density, long cycle life, and reliability. They offer a higher driving range than traditional internal combustion engine vehicles and are more environmentally friendly. The energy density of graphite-based LIBs is currently around 260 W h kg<small><sup>1</sup></small>. The battery industry aims to achieve an energy density of 500 W h kg<small><sup>1</sup></small>, but the graphite anode has reached its theoretical capacity limit. They are also limited by other dilemmas, such as fast charging that can cause lithium plating and safety hazards, an inability to be used at high rates, and severe performance degradation. Therefore, it can be affirmed that HC-LIBs will play an important role in the future battery application field. We summarize some of the research data on HC anodes in LIBs in <a title="Select to navigate to table" href="#tab1">Table 1</a>, which shows that the capacity is very high and can even reach up to five times the theoretical capacity of graphite (372 mA h g<small><sup>1</sup></small>). However, it has also been found that their initial cycle capacity loss is as high as 20% to 50%. The low ICE results in a very low energy density in a full-cell, which is probably the reason why full-cell data is rarely reported in research papers on HC-LIBs. However, full-cell validation is necessary and we strongly recommend that future work is compiled to validate full-cells.</span>
        <br><div class="image_table"><table><tbody><tr><td colspan="3" class="imgHolder" id="imgfig18"><a href="/image/article/2023/EE/d3ee02202a/d3ee02202a-f18_hi-res.gif" title="Select to open image in new window" onclick="open(this.href, &quot;_blank&quot;, &quot;toolbar=1,scrollbars=yes,resizable=1&quot;); return false;"><img alt="image file: d3ee02202a-f18.tif" src="/image/article/2023/EE/d3ee02202a/d3ee02202a-f18.gif"></a></td></tr><tr><td class="pushTitleRight"> </td><td class="image_title"><b>Fig. 18 </b> <span id="fig18"><span class="graphic_title">Current limitations of graphite-based LIBs and merits of developing HC-based LIBs.</span></span></td><td class="pushTitleLeft"> </td></tr></tbody></table></div>
